href="/wiki/Indo-European_Etymological_Dictionary" title="Indo-European Etymological Dictionary">Indo-European Etymological Dictionary</a></i></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Indo-European_topics" title="Template:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indo-European_topics" title="Template talk:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indo-European_topics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width:1px"></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains characters used to write reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> words.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> combining characters and <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a> characters.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Proto-Indo-European mythology</b> is the body of myths and stories associated with the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-Europeans</a>. Although these stories are not directly attested, they have been reconstructed by scholars of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_mythology" title="Comparative mythology">comparative mythology</a> based on the similarities in the belief systems of various Indo-European peoples.\n</p><p>Various schools of thought exist regarding the precise nature of Proto-Indo-European mythology, which do not always agree with each other.  The main mythologies used in comparative reconstruction are <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse</a>, often supported with evidence from the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_mythology" title="Baltic mythology">Baltic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology" title="Celtic mythology">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_mythology" title="Hittite mythology">Hittite</a> traditions as well.\n</p><p>The Proto-Indo-European pantheon includes well-attested deities such as <a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">*<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i></a>, the god of the daylit skies, his daughter <a href="/wiki/Hausos" title="Hausos">*<i>H<sub>a</sub>&#233;us&#333;s</i></a>, the goddess of the dawn, the <a href="/wiki/Divine_twins" title="Divine twins">divine twins</a>, and the storm god <a href="/wiki/Perkwunos" title="Perkwunos">*<i>Perk<sup>w</sup>unos</i></a>. Other probable deities include <i>*P&#233;h<sub>2</sub>us&#333;n</i>, a pastoral god, and <i>*Seh<sub>2</sub>ul</i>, a female <a href="/wiki/Solar_deity" title="Solar deity">solar deity</a>.\n</p><p>Well-attested myths of the Proto-Indo-Europeans include a myth involving a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_god" title="Storm god">storm god</a> who slays a multi-headed serpent that dwells in water and a creation story involving two brothers, one of whom sacrifices the other to create the world. The Proto-Indo-Europeans may have believed that the <a href="/wiki/Otherworld" title="Otherworld">Otherworld</a> was guarded by a watchdog and could only be reached by crossing a river. They also may have believed in a world tree, bearing fruit of immortality, either guarded by or gnawed on by a serpent or dragon, and tended by three goddesses who spun the thread of life.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Methods_of_reconstruction"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Methods of reconstruction</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Schools_of_thought"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Schools of thought</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Source_mythologies"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Source mythologies</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Pantheon"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Pantheon</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Heavenly_deities"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Heavenly deities</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6"><a href="#Sky_Father"><span class="tocnumber">2.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Sky Father</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#Dawn_Goddess"><span class="tocnumber">2.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Dawn Goddess</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-8"><a href="#Sun_and_Moon"><span class="tocnumber">2.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Sun and Moon</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Divine_Twins"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Divine Twins</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-10"><a href="#Horse_Twins"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Horse Twins</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Twin_Founders"><span class="tocnumber">2.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Twin Founders</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Storm_deities"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Storm deities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Water_deities"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Water deities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Nature_deities"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Nature deities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Societal_deities"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Societal deities</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Mythology"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Mythology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Dragon_or_serpent"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Dragon or serpent</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Twin_founders_2"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Twin founders</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Fire_in_water"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Fire in water</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Binding_of_evil"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Binding of evil</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Cosmogony"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Cosmogony</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Cosmology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Cosmology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Underworld"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Underworld</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#World_tree_and_serpent"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">World tree and serpent</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_reconstruction">Methods of reconstruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Methods of reconstruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_of_thought">Schools of thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Schools of thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Max_Muller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="874" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/220px-Max_Muller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/330px-Max_Muller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/440px-Max_Muller.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Max_Muller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max M&#252;ller">Friedrich Max M&#252;ller</a>, a prominent early scholar on the reconstruction of Proto-Indo-European religion and a proponent of the Meteorological School<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198713&#8211;15_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198713&#8211;15-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The mythology of the Proto-Indo-Europeans is not directly attested and it is difficult to match their language to archaeological findings related to any specific culture from the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006-3">[3]</a></sup> Nonetheless, scholars of comparative mythology have attempted to reconstruct aspects of Proto-Indo-European mythology based on the existence of similarities among the <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>, religious practices, and myths of various Indo-European peoples. This method is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">comparative method</a>. Different schools of thought have approached the subject of Proto-Indo-European mythology from different angles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006427&#8211;431_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006427&#8211;431-4">[4]</a></sup> The Meteorological School holds that Proto-Indo-European mythology was largely centered around deified natural phenomena such as the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dawn" title="Dawn">dawn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428-5">[5]</a></sup> This meteorological interpretation was popular among early scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max M&#252;ller">Friedrich Max M&#252;ller</a>, who saw all myths as fundamentally solar allegories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198713&#8211;15_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198713&#8211;15-2">[2]</a></sup> This school lost most of its scholarly support in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714&#8211;15_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714&#8211;15-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Ritual School, which first became prominent in the late nineteenth century, holds that Proto-Indo-European myths are best understood as stories invented to explain various rituals and religious practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428&#8211;429_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428&#8211;429-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714&#8211;15_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714&#8211;15-6">[6]</a></sup> The Ritual School reached the height of its popularity during the early twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198715&#8211;18_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198715&#8211;18-8">[8]</a></sup> Many of its most prominent early proponents, such as <a href="/wiki/James_George_Frazer" title="James George Frazer">James George Frazer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jane_Ellen_Harrison" title="Jane Ellen Harrison">Jane Ellen Harrison</a>, were classical scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198715_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198715-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lincoln" title="Bruce Lincoln">Bruce Lincoln</a>, a contemporary member of the Ritual School, argues that the Proto-Indo-Europeans believed that every sacrifice was a reenactment of the original sacrifice performed by the founder of the human race on his twin brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428&#8211;429_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006428&#8211;429-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Functionalist School holds that Proto-Indo-European society and, consequently, their mythology, was largely centered around the <a href="/wiki/Trifunctional_hypothesis" title="Trifunctional hypothesis">trifunctional system</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dum&#233;zil">Georges Dum&#233;zil</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006429&#8211;430_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006429&#8211;430-10">[10]</a></sup> which holds that Proto-Indo-European society was divided into three distinct social classes: farmers, warriors, and priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006429&#8211;430_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006429&#8211;430-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkert198518_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkert198518-12">[12]</a></sup> The Structuralist School, by contrast, argues that Proto-Indo-European mythology was largely centered around the concept of <a href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dualistic</a> opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup> This approach generally tends to focus on cultural universals within the realm of mythology, rather than the genetic origins of those myths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup> but it also offers refinements of the Dum&#233;zilian trifunctional system by highlighting the oppositional elements present within each function, such as the creative and destructive elements both found within the role of the warrior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_mythologies">Source mythologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Source mythologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IE_expansion.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="275" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/IE_expansion.png/330px-IE_expansion.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/IE_expansion.png 1.5x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IE_expansion.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scheme of Indo-European migrations from c. 4000 to 1000 BC according to the <a href="/wiki/Kurgan_hypothesis" title="Kurgan hypothesis">Kurgan hypothesis</a></div></div></div>\n<p>One of the earliest attested and thus most important of all Indo-European mythologies is <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic mythology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440-14">[14]</a></sup> especially the mythology of the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, the oldest of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>. Early scholars of comparative mythology such as Friedrich Max M&#252;ller stressed the importance of Vedic mythology to such an extent that they practically equated it with Proto-Indo-European myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714-15">[15]</a></sup> Modern researchers have been much more cautious, recognizing that, although Vedic mythology is still central, other mythologies must also be taken into account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel198714-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another of the most important source mythologies for comparative research is <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987191_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987191-16">[16]</a></sup> Contrary to the frequent erroneous statement made by some authors that "Rome has no myth", the Romans possessed a very complex mythological system, parts of which have been preserved through the characteristic Roman tendency to rationalize their myths into historical accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987146&#8211;147_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987146&#8211;147-17">[17]</a></sup> Despite its relatively late attestation, <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a> is still considered one of the three most important of the Indo-European mythologies for comparative research,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440-14">[14]</a></sup> simply due to the vast bulk of surviving Icelandic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987191_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987191-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Baltic_mythology" title="Baltic mythology">Baltic mythology</a> has also received a great deal of scholarly attention, but has so far remained frustrating to researchers because the sources are so comparatively late.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987223&#8211;228_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987223&#8211;228-18">[18]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Latvian folk songs are seen as a major source of information in the process of reconstructing Proto-Indo-European myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987228&#8211;229_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987228&#8211;229-19">[19]</a></sup> Despite the popularity of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> in western culture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987126-127_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987126-127-20">[20]</a></sup> Greek mythology is generally seen as having little importance in comparative mythology due to the heavy influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Greek" title="Pre-Greek">Pre-Greek</a> and Near Eastern cultures, which overwhelms what little Indo-European material can be extracted from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987138,_143_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987138,_143-21">[21]</a></sup> Consequently, Greek mythology received minimal scholarly attention until the mid 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006440-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> are considered relatively conservative in regards to Proto-Indo-European cultures, retaining a similar lifestyle and culture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scythian_religion" title="Scythian religion">their mythology</a> has very rarely been examined in an Indo-European context and infrequently discussed in regards to the nature of the ancestral Indo-European mythology. At least three deities, <a href="/wiki/Tabiti" title="Tabiti">Tabiti</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Papaios&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Papaios (page does not exist)">Papaios</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Api" title="Api">Api</a>, are generally interpreted as having Indo-European origins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> while the remaining have seen more disparate interpretations. Influence from Siberian, Turkic and even Near Eastern beliefs, on the other hand, are more widely discussed in literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pantheon">Pantheon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pantheon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Linguists are able to reconstruct the names of some deities in the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a> (PIE) from many types of sources. Some of the proposed deity names are more readily accepted among scholars than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[a]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term for "a god" was <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/deyw%C3%B3s" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/deyw&#243;s">*deiwos</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> reflected in Hittite, <i>sius</i>; Latin, <i>deus</i>, <i>divus</i>; Sanskrit, <i><a href="/wiki/Dyaus" title="Dyaus">Dyaus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deva_(Hinduism)" title="Deva (Hinduism)">deva</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">Avestan</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Daeva" title="Daeva">daeva</a></i> (later, Persian, <i>div</i>); Welsh, <i>duw</i>; Irish, <i>dia</i>; Lithuanian, <i><a href="/wiki/Dievas" title="Dievas">Dievas</a></i>; Latvian, <i>Dievs</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heavenly_deities">Heavenly deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Heavenly deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sky_Father">Sky Father</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sky Father">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sky_father" title="Sky father">Sky father</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/220px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/330px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/440px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Laurel_wreath" title="Laurel wreath">Laurel-wreathed</a> head of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> on a gold <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">stater</a> from the Greek city of <a href="/wiki/Lampsacus" title="Lampsacus">Lampsacus</a>, c 360&#8211;340 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The head deity of the Proto-Indo-European pantheon was the god <a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">*<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409,_431&#8211;432_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409,_431&#8211;432-31">[30]</a></sup> whose name literally means "Sky Father".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409,_431&#8211;432_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409,_431&#8211;432-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinters2003134&#8211;135_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinters2003134&#8211;135-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkert198517_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkert198517-33">[32]</a></sup> He is believed to have been regarded as the god of the daylit skies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007166&#8211;168_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007166&#8211;168-34">[33]</a></sup> He is, by far, the most well-attested of all the Proto-Indo-European deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007166_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007166-35">[34]</a></sup> The Greek god <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, the Roman god <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, and the Illyrian god Dei-P&#225;trous all appear as the head gods of their respective pantheons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987198&#8211;200_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987198&#8211;200-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkert198517_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkert198517-33">[32]</a></sup> The Norse god <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr" title="T&#253;r">T&#253;r</a>, however, seems to have been demoted to the role of a minor war-deity prior to the composition of the earliest Germanic texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987198&#8211;200_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987198&#8211;200-36">[35]</a></sup> *<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i> is also attested in the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyaus_Pita" title="Dyaus Pita">Dy&#225;us Pit&#257;</a>, a minor ancestor figure mentioned in only a few hymns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409_and_431_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006409_and_431-37">[36]</a></sup> The names of the Latvian god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dievs" title="Dievs">Dievs</a> and the Hittite god Attas Isanus do not preserve the exact literal translation of the name *<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup> but do preserve the general meaning of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006431-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>*Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i> may have had a consort who was an earth goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007181_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007181-38">[37]</a></sup> This possibility is attested in the Vedic pairing of Dy&#225;us Pit&#257; and <a href="/wiki/Prithvi" title="Prithvi">Prithvi Mater</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007181_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007181-38">[37]</a></sup> the Roman pairing of Jupiter and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tellus_Mater" title="Tellus Mater">Tellus Mater</a> from <a href="/wiki/Macrobius" title="Macrobius">Macrobius</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Saturnalia_(Macrobius)" title="Saturnalia (Macrobius)">Saturnalia</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007181_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007181-38">[37]</a></sup> and the Norse pairing of <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6r%C3%B0" title="J&#246;r&#240;">J&#246;r&#240;</a>. Odin is not a reflex of *<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i>, but his cult may have subsumed aspects of an earlier chief deity who was.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007183_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007183-39">[38]</a></sup> This pairing may also be further attested in an Old English ploughing prayer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007183_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007183-39">[38]</a></sup> and in the Greek pairings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ouranos" title="Ouranos">Ouranos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> and Zeus and <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007181&#8211;183_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007181&#8211;183-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dawn_Goddess">Dawn Goddess</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dawn Goddess">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1921" data-file-width="2637" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg/220px-Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg/330px-Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg/440px-Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eos_chariot_430-420_BC_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eos" title="Eos">Eos</a> in her chariot flying over the sea, red-figure <a href="/wiki/Krater" title="Krater">krater</a> from South <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, 430&#8211;420 BC, <a href="/wiki/Staatliche_Antikensammlungen" title="Staatliche Antikensammlungen">Staatliche Antikensammlungen</a>, Munich</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hausos" title="Hausos">*<i>H<sub>a</sub>&#233;us&#333;s</i></a> has been reconstructed as the Proto-Indo-European goddess of the dawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410,_432_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410,_432-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-227_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-227-42">[41]</a></sup> Twenty-one hymns in the Rigveda are dedicated to the dawn goddess <a href="/wiki/Ushas" title="Ushas">U&#7779;&#225;s</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> and a single passage from the Avesta honors the dawn goddess U&#353;&#229;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> The dawn goddess <a href="/wiki/Eos" title="Eos">Eos</a> appears prominently in early Greek poetry and mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> The Roman dawn goddess <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(mythology)" title="Aurora (mythology)">Aurora</a> is a reflection of the Greek Eos,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> but the original Roman dawn goddess may have continued to be worshipped under the cultic title <a href="/wiki/Mater_Matuta" title="Mater Matuta">Mater Matuta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxons worshipped the goddess <a href="/wiki/%C4%92ostre" title="&#274;ostre">&#274;ostre</a>, who was associated with a festival in spring which later gave its name to a month, which gave its name to the Christian holiday of <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217-43">[42]</a></sup> The name <i>&#212;starm&#226;n&#244;th</i> in <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> has been taken as an indication that a similar goddess was also worshipped in southern Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007217&#8211;218_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007217&#8211;218-44">[43]</a></sup> The Lithuanian dawn goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrine" title="Austrine">Au&#353;ra</a> was still acknowledged in the sixteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007218_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007218-45">[44]</a></sup> U&#7779;&#225;s in the Sanskrit tradition and Eos in the Greek have very similar attributes, indicating that these attributes were established by at least the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Aryan" title="Greco-Aryan">Greco-Aryan</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007218&#8211;224_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007218&#8211;224-46">[45]</a></sup> Both goddesses are also portrayed as taking mortal lovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007224&#8211;225_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007224&#8211;225-47">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sun_and_Moon">Sun and Moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sun and Moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="862" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg/220px-HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg/330px-HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg/440px-HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HittiteGoddessAndChildAnatolia15th-13thCenturyBCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Possible depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Sun_goddess_of_Arinna" title="Sun goddess of Arinna">Hittite Sun goddess</a> holding a child in her arms from between 1400 and 1200 BC</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_deity" title="Sun deity"><i>*Seh<sub>2</sub>ul</i></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_deity" title="Moon deity"><i>*Meh<sub>1</sub>not</i></a> are reconstructed as the Proto-Indo-European goddess of the Sun and god of the Moon respectively. <i>*Seh<sub>2</sub>ul</i> is reconstructed based on the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, the Roman god <a href="/wiki/Sol_(mythology)" title="Sol (mythology)">Sol</a>, the Celtic goddess <a href="/wiki/Sulis" title="Sulis">Sul/Suil</a>, the North Germanic goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%B3l_(Sun)" title="S&#243;l (Sun)">S&#243;l</a>, the Continental Germanic goddess <a href="/wiki/Sowil%C5%8D" title="Sowil&#333;">*Sowil&#333;</a>, the Hittite goddess <a href="/wiki/Sun_goddess_of_Arinna" title="Sun goddess of Arinna">"UTU-liya"</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760-48">[47]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Hvare-khshaeta" title="Hvare-khshaeta">Hvare-khshaeta</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760-48">[47]</a></sup> and the Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997232_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997232-49">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>*Meh<sub>1</sub>not-</i> is reconstructed based on the Norse god <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1ni" title="M&#225;ni">M&#225;ni</a>, the Slavic god <a href="/wiki/Jarilo" title="Jarilo">Myesyats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGamkrelidzeIvanov1995760-48">[47]</a></sup> and the Lithuanian god *<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Lithuanian_gods#Gods_and_god-like_beings" title="List of Lithuanian gods">Meno</a>, or M&#279;nuo (M&#279;nulis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997385_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997385-50">[49]</a></sup> They are often seen as the twin children of various deities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> but in fact the sun and moon were deified several times and are often found in competing forms within the same language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The usual scheme is that one of these celestial deities is male and the other female, though the exact gender of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> tends to vary among subsequent Indo-European mythologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> The original Indo-European solar deity appears to have been female,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> a characteristic not only supported by the higher number of sun goddesses in subsequent derivations (feminine S&#243;l, Saule, <a href="/wiki/Sulis" title="Sulis">Sulis</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ta%C3%ADn" title="&#201;ta&#237;n">&#201;ta&#237;n</a>, Gr&#237;an, <a href="/wiki/Aimend" title="Aimend">Aimend</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ine" title="&#193;ine">&#193;ine</a>, and Catha versus masculine Helios, Surya, <a href="/wiki/Savitr" title="Savitr">Savitr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Usil" title="Usil">Usil</a>, and Sol) (<a href="/wiki/Hvare-khshaeta" title="Hvare-khshaeta">Hvare-khshaeta</a> is of neutral gender),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> but also by vestiges in mythologies with male solar deities (Usil in Etruscan art is depicted occasionally as a goddess, while solar characteristics in <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helen_of_Troy" title="Helen of Troy">Helen of Troy</a> still remain in Greek mythology).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> The original Indo-European lunar deity appears to have been masculine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> with feminine lunar deities like Selene, Minerva, and Luna being a development exclusive to the eastern Mediterranean. Even in these traditions, remnants of male lunar deities, like <a href="/wiki/Menelaus" title="Menelaus">Menelaus</a>, remain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the sun was personified as an independent, female deity, the Proto-Indo-Europeans also visualized the sun as the eye of <a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">*<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i></a>, as seen in various reflexes: <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a> as the eye of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hvare-khshaeta" title="Hvare-khshaeta">Hvare-khshaeta</a> as the eye of <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a>, and the sun as "God\'s eye" in Romanian folklore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> The names of Celtic sun goddesses like <a href="/wiki/Sulis" title="Sulis">Sulis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grian" title="Grian">Grian</a> may also allude to this association; the words for "eye" and "sun" are switched in these languages, hence the name of the goddesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_Twins">Divine Twins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Divine Twins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Horse_Twins">Horse Twins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Horse Twins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2450" data-file-width="3266" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg/300px-Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg/450px-Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg/600px-Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dioskouroi_Met_L.2008.18.1-2_n03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pair of Roman statuettes from the third century AD depicting the Dioscuri as horsemen, with their characteristic skullcaps (<a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_Twins" title="Divine Twins">Horse Twins</a> are a set of twin brothers found throughout nearly every Indo-European pantheon who usually have a name that means \'horse\' <i>*ekwa-</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432-64">[63]</a></sup> but the names are not always cognate and no Proto-Indo-European name for them can be reconstructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432-64">[63]</a></sup> In most Indo-European pantheons, the Horse Twins are brothers of the Sun Maiden or Dawn goddess, and sons of the sky god.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007185&#8211;191_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007185&#8211;191-65">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>They are reconstructed based on the Vedic <a href="/wiki/Ashvins" title="Ashvins">Ashvins</a>, the Lithuanian <a href="/wiki/A%C5%A1vieniai" title="A&#353;vieniai">A&#353;vieniai</a>, the Latvian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dieva_deli" title="Dieva deli">Dieva deli</a>, the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioskouroi" title="Dioskouroi">Dioskouroi</a> (Kastor and Polydeukes), the Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscuri" title="Dioscuri">Dioscuri</a> (Castor and Pollux), and the Old English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hengist" title="Hengist">Hengist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsa" title="Horsa">Horsa</a> (whose names mean "stallion" and "horse").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007186&#8211;191_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007186&#8211;191-66">[65]</a></sup> References from the Greek writer <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(historian)" title="Timaeus (historian)">Timaeus</a> indicate that the Celts may have had a set of horse twins as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007190_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007190-67">[66]</a></sup> The Welsh <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%A2n_the_Blessed" title="Br&#226;n the Blessed">Br&#226;n</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manawydan" title="Manawydan">Manawydan</a> may also be related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006432-64">[63]</a></sup> The horse twins may have been based on the morning and evening star (the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>) and they often have stories about them in which they "accompany" the Sun goddess, because of the close orbit of the planet Venus to the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Twin_Founders">Twin Founders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Twin Founders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Proto-Indo-European Creation myth seems to have involved two key figures: *<i>Manu-</i> ("Man"; Indic <a href="/wiki/Manu_(Hinduism)" title="Manu (Hinduism)">Manu</a>; Germanic <a href="/wiki/Mannus" title="Mannus">Mannus</a>) and his twin brother *<i>Yemo-</i> ("Twin"; Indic <a href="/wiki/Yama" title="Yama">Yama</a>; Germanic <a href="/wiki/Ymir" title="Ymir">Ymir</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991140_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMallory1991140-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135-70">[69]</a></sup> Reflexes of these two figures usually fulfill the respective roles of founder of the human race and first human to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMallory1991140_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMallory1991140-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELincoln1991_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELincoln1991-71">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storm_deities">Storm deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Storm deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="953" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg/170px-Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg/255px-Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg/340px-Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taranis_Jupiter_with_wheel_and_thunderbolt_Le_Chatelet_Gourzon_Haute_Marne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman" title="Gallo-Roman">Gallo-Roman</a> statue of the storm-god <a href="/wiki/Taranis" title="Taranis">Taranis</a>, clutching a wheel and thunderbolt, from Le Chatelet, Gourzon, <a href="/wiki/Haute-Marne" title="Haute-Marne">Haute-Marne</a>, France</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Perkwunos" title="Perkwunos">*<i>Perk<sup>w</sup>unos</i></a> has been reconstructed as the Proto-Indo-European god of lightning and storms. His name literally means "The Striker." He is reconstructed based on the Norse goddess <a href="/wiki/Fj%C3%B6rgyn_and_Fj%C3%B6rgynn" title="Fj&#246;rgyn and Fj&#246;rgynn">Fj&#491;rgyn</a> (the mother of <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>), the Lithuanian god <a href="/wiki/Perk%C5%ABnas" title="Perk&#363;nas">Perk&#363;nas</a>, and the Slavic god <a href="/wiki/Perun" title="Perun">Per&#250;n&#250;</a>. The Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Parjanya" title="Parjanya">Parj&#225;nya</a> may also be related, but his possible connection to *<i>Perk<sup>w</sup>unos</i> is still under dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410_and_433_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410_and_433-72">[71]</a></sup> The name of *<i>Perk<sup>w</sup>unos</i> may also be attested in Greek as <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BA%CE%B5%CF%81%CE%B1%CF%85%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:&#954;&#949;&#961;&#945;&#965;&#957;&#972;&#962;">&#954;&#949;&#961;&#945;&#965;&#957;&#972;&#962;</a></i> (<i>Keraun&#243;s</i>), an epithet of the god <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> meaning "thunder-shaker."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987235_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987235-73">[72]</a></sup> A possible alternative name, through the root *(s)tenh&#8322;, is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> as well as Hittite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarhunt" title="Tarhunt">Tarhunt</a> and Celtic <a href="/wiki/Taranis" title="Taranis">Taran/Taranis</a>. The Roman god <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> is also a speculated descendent, since he originally had thunderer characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_deities">Water deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Water deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some authors have proposed <i>*Neptonos</i> or *<i>H<sub>2</sub>epom Nep&#333;ts</i> as the Proto-Indo-European god of the waters. The name literally means "Grandson [or <i>Nephew</i>] of the Waters."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410-77">[76]</a></sup> Philologists reconstruct his name from that of the Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Apam_Napat" title="Apam Napat">Ap&#225;m N&#225;p&#225;t</a>, the Roman god <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Nept&#363;nus</a>, and the Old Irish god <a href="/wiki/Nechtan_(mythology)" title="Nechtan (mythology)">Nechtain</a>. Although such a god has been solidly reconstructed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_religion" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian religion">Proto-Indo-Iranian religion</a>, Mallory and Adams nonetheless still reject him as a Proto-Indo-European deity on linguistic grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410-77">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A river goddess *<i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/d%C3%A9h%E2%82%82nu" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/d&#233;h&#8322;nu">Deh<sub>a</sub>nu-</a></i> has been proposed based on the Vedic goddess <a href="/wiki/Danu_(Asura)" title="Danu (Asura)">D&#257;nu</a>, the Irish goddess <a href="/wiki/Danu_(Irish_goddess)" title="Danu (Irish goddess)">Danu</a>, the Welsh goddess Don and the names of the rivers <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, Don, Dnieper, and Dniester. Mallory and Adams, however, dismiss this reconstruction, commenting that it does not have any evidence to support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006434_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006434-78">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some have also proposed the reconstruction of a sea god named *<i>Trih<sub>a</sub>t&#333;n</i> based on the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Triton_(mythology)" title="Triton (mythology)">Triton</a> and the Old Irish word <i>tr&#239;ath</i>, meaning "sea." Mallory and Adams reject this reconstruction as having no basis, asserting that the "lexical correspondence is only just possible and with no evidence of a cognate sea god in Irish."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006434_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006434-78">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_deities">Nature deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Nature deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:506px;max-width:506px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Two similar depictions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horned_deities" title="Horned deities">horned deities</a> from the Celtic and Indic traditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-80">[79]</a></sup></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:300px;max-width:300px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1884" data-file-width="2768" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg/298px-Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg/447px-Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg/596px-Gundestrupkedlen-_00054_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="298"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Detail from the <a href="/wiki/Gundestrup_cauldron" title="Gundestrup cauldron">Gundestrup cauldron</a> from Gundestrup, Denmark, thought to date between 150 BC and 1 AD, showing the Celtic god <a href="/wiki/Cernunnos" title="Cernunnos">Cernunnos</a> with horns, sitting in a meditative position, surrounded by animals</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiva_Pashupati.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="926" data-file-width="920" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/200px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/300px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/400px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Pashupati_seal" title="Pashupati seal">Pashupati seal</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a> in northern India, dated to between 2350 and 2000 BC, showing a horned, <a href="/wiki/Polycephaly" title="Polycephaly">tricephelic</a> deity in a meditative position, surrounded by animals</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p><i>*P&#233;h<sub>2</sub>us&#333;n</i>, a pastoral deity, is reconstructed based on the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Pan_(god)" title="Pan (god)">Pan</a> and the Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Pushan" title="Pushan">P&#363;sh&#257;n</a>. Both deities are closely affiliated with goats and were worshipped as pastoral deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006411_and_434_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006411_and_434-81">[80]</a></sup> The minor discrepancies between the two deities can be easily explained by the possibility that many attributes originally associated with Pan may have been transferred over to his father <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006411_and_434_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006411_and_434-81">[80]</a></sup> The association between Pan and P&#363;sh&#257;n was first identified in 1924 by the German scholar <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Collitz" title="Hermann Collitz">Hermann Collitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1855, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adalbert_Kuhn" title="Adalbert Kuhn">Adalbert Kuhn</a> suggested that the Proto-Indo-Europeans may have believed in a set of helper deities, whom he reconstructed based on the Germanic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elves" title="Elves">elves</a> and the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Ribhus" title="Ribhus">ribhus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhn1855-p110_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhn1855-p110-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Though this proposal is often mentioned in academic writings, very few scholars actually accept it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> There may also have been a female cognate akin to the Greco-Roman <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">nymphs</a>, Slavic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_fairies" title="Slavic fairies">vilas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huldra" title="Huldra">Huldra</a> of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic folklore</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apsaras" title="Apsaras">Apsaras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007284&#8211;292_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007284&#8211;292-87">[86]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_deities">Societal deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Societal deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2524" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg/290px-Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg/435px-Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg/580px-Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paphos_Haus_des_Theseus_-_Mosaik_Achilles_3_Moiren.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late second-century AD Greek mosaic from the <a href="/wiki/Paphos_Archaeological_Park#House_of_Theseus" title="Paphos Archaeological Park">House of Theseus</a> at <a href="/wiki/Paphos_Archaeological_Park" title="Paphos Archaeological Park">Paphos Archaeological Park</a> on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> showing the three Moirai: <a href="/wiki/Clotho" title="Clotho">Klotho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lachesis" title="Lachesis">Lachesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atropos" title="Atropos">Atropos</a>, standing behind <a href="/wiki/Peleus" title="Peleus">Peleus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thetis" title="Thetis">Thetis</a>, the parents of <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a></div></div></div>\n<p>It is highly probable that the Proto-Indo-Europeans believed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_goddesses" title="Triple goddesses">three fate goddesses</a> who <a href="/wiki/Spinning_(textiles)" title="Spinning (textiles)">spun</a> the destinies of mankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380&#8211;385_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380&#8211;385-88">[87]</a></sup> Although such fate goddesses are not directly attested in the Indo-Aryan tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a> does contain an allusion comparing fate to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warp_and_woof" title="Warp and woof">warp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380-89">[88]</a></sup> Furthermore, the three Fates appear in nearly every other Indo-European mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380-89">[88]</a></sup> The earliest attested set of fate goddesses are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulses" title="Gulses">Gulses</a> in Hittite mythology, who were said to preside over the individual destinies of human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380-89">[88]</a></sup> They often appear in mythical narratives alongside the goddesses Papaya and Istustaya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380-89">[88]</a></sup> who, in a ritual text for the foundation of a new temple, are described sitting holding mirrors and spindles, spinning the king\'s thread of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380_89-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380-89">[88]</a></sup> In the Greek tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Moirai" title="Moirai">Moirai</a> ("Apportioners") are mentioned dispensing destiny in both the <i>Iliad</i> and the <i>Odyssey</i>, in which they are given the epithet &#922;&#955;&#8182;&#952;&#949;&#962; (<i>Klothes</i>, meaning "Spinners").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007380&#8211;381_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007380&#8211;381-91">[90]</a></sup> In Hesiod\'s <i>Theogony</i>, the Moirai are said to "give mortal men both good and ill" and their names are listed as <a href="/wiki/Clotho" title="Clotho">Klotho</a> ("Spinner"), <a href="/wiki/Lachesis" title="Lachesis">Lachesis</a> ("Apportioner"), and <a href="/wiki/Atropos" title="Atropos">Atropos</a> ("Inflexible").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007381_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007381-93">[92]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> records that Klotho sings of the past, Lachesis of the present, and Atropos of the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup> In Roman legend, the <a href="/wiki/Parcae" title="Parcae">Parcae</a> were three goddesses who presided over the births of children and whose names were Nona ("Ninth"), Decuma ("Tenth"), and Morta ("Death").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007381_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007381-93">[92]</a></sup> They too were said to spin destinies, although this may have been due to influence from Greek literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007381_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007381-93">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Old Norse <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lusp%C3%A1" title="V&#246;lusp&#225;">V&#246;lusp&#225;</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gylfaginning" title="Gylfaginning">Gylfaginning</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Norns" title="Norns">Norns</a> are three cosmic goddesses of fate who are described sitting by the well of <a href="/wiki/Ur%C3%B0r" title="Ur&#240;r">Ur&#240;r</a> at the foot of the world tree <a href="/wiki/Yggdrasil" title="Yggdrasil">Yggdrasil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007382_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007382-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[b]</a></sup> In Old Norse texts, the Norns are frequently conflated with <a href="/wiki/Valkyrie" title="Valkyrie">Valkyries</a>, who are sometimes also described as spinning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007382_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007382-96">[95]</a></sup> Old English texts, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rhyming_Poem" title="The Rhyming Poem">Rhyme Poem</a></i> 70, and <i><a href="/wiki/Guthlac_of_Crowland" title="Guthlac of Crowland">Guthlac</a></i> 1350 f., reference <a href="/wiki/Wyrd" title="Wyrd">Wyrd</a> as a singular power that "weaves" destinies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007382&#8211;383_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007382&#8211;383-98">[96]</a></sup> Later texts mention the Wyrds as a group, with <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> referring to them as "the Werdys that we clepyn Destin&#233;" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Good_Women" title="The Legend of Good Women">The Legend of Good Women</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[c]</a></sup> A goddess spinning appears in a <a href="/wiki/Bracteate" title="Bracteate">bracteate</a> from southwest Germany<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup> and a relief from <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a> shows three mother goddesses, with two of them holding distaffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup> Tenth-century German ecclesiastical writings denounce the popular belief in three sisters who determined the course of a man\'s life at his birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup> An Old Irish hymn attests to seven goddesses who were believed to weave the thread of destiny, which demonstrates that these spinster fate-goddesses were present in Celtic mythology as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384-101">[98]</a></sup> A Lithuanian folktale recorded in 1839 recounts that a man\'s fate is spun at his birth by seven goddesses known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deives_Valdytojos" title="Deives Valdytojos">deiv&#279;s valdytojos</a></i> and used to hang a star in the sky;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384-101">[98]</a></sup> when he dies, his thread snaps and his star falls as a meteor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384-101">[98]</a></sup> In Latvian folk songs, a goddess called the <a href="/wiki/Laima" title="Laima">L&#225;ima</a> is described as weaving a child\'s fate at its birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384-101">[98]</a></sup> Although she is usually only one goddess, the L&#225;ima sometimes appears as three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384_101-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384-101">[98]</a></sup> The three spinning fate goddesses appear in Slavic traditions in the forms of the Russian Ro&#382;anicy, the Czech Sudi&#269;ky, the Bulgarian Naren&#269;nice or Urisnice, the Polish Rodzanice, the Croatian Rodjenice, the Serbian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sudjenice" title="Sudjenice">Sudjenice</a>, and the Slovene Rojenice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007384&#8211;385_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007384&#8211;385-102">[99]</a></sup> Albanian folk tales speak of the <a href="/wiki/Fatit" title="Fatit">Fatit</a>, three old women who appear three days after a child is born and determine its fate, using language reminiscent of spinning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007385_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007385-103">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franks_Casket_vorne_links.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4b/Franks_Casket_vorne_links.jpg" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franks_Casket_vorne_links.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <a href="/wiki/Wayland_the_Smith" title="Wayland the Smith">Wayland the Smith</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Franks_Casket" title="Franks Casket">Franks Casket</a>, dating to the eighth century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Although the name of a particular Proto-Indo-European smith god cannot be linguistically reconstructed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410-77">[76]</a></sup> it is highly probable that the Proto-Indo-Europeans had a smith deity of some kind, since smith gods occur in nearly every Indo-European culture, with examples including the Hittite god Hasammili, the Vedic god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tvastr" title="Tvastr">Tvastr</a>, the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>, the Germanic villain <a href="/wiki/Wayland_the_Smith" title="Wayland the Smith">Wayland the Smith</a>, and the Ossetian culture figure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdalagon" title="Kurdalagon">Kurdalagon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2009154&#8211;157_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2009154&#8211;157-104">[101]</a></sup> Many of these smith figures share certain characteristics in common. Hephaestus, the Greek god of blacksmiths, and Wayland the Smith, a nefarious blacksmith from Germanic mythology, are both described as lame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2009_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2009-105">[102]</a></sup> Additionally, Wayland the Smith and the Greek mythical inventor <a href="/wiki/Daedalus" title="Daedalus">Daedalus</a> both escape imprisonment on an island by fashioning sets of mechanical wings from feathers and wax and using them to fly away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2009155_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2009155-106">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Proto-Indo-Europeans may have had a goddess who presided over the <a href="/wiki/Trifunctional_hypothesis" title="Trifunctional hypothesis">trifunctional</a> organization of society. Various epithets of the Iranian goddess <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a> and the Roman goddess <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> provide sufficient evidence to solidly attest that she was probably worshipped, but no specific name for her can be lexically reconstructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams433_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams433-107">[104]</a></sup> Vague remnants of this goddess may also be preserved in the Greek goddess <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987133&#8211;134_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987133&#8211;134-108">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some scholars have proposed a war god *<i>M&#257;wort-</i> based on the Roman god <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a> and the Vedic <a href="/wiki/Maruts" title="Maruts">Marut&#225;s</a>, companions of the war-god <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>. Mallory and Adams, however, reject this reconstruction on linguistic grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410&#8211;411_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006410&#8211;411-109">[106]</a></sup> Likewise, some researchers have found it more plausible that Mars was originally a storm deity, while this cannot be said for Ares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mythology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dragon_or_serpent">Dragon or serpent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dragon or serpent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaoskampf" title="Chaoskampf">Chaoskampf</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="1279" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/450px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/675px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg/900px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations082_kopie1jpg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hittite god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarhunt" title="Tarhunt">Tarhunt</a>, followed by his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarruma" title="Sarruma">Sarruma</a>, kills the dragon <a href="/wiki/Illuyanka" title="Illuyanka">Illuyanka</a> (Museum of Anatolian Civilizations, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ankara,_Turkey" title="Ankara, Turkey">Ankara, Turkey</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>One common myth found in nearly all Indo-European mythologies is a battle ending with a <a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">hero</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gods" title="Gods">god</a> slaying a <a href="/wiki/Serpent_(symbolism)" title="Serpent (symbolism)">serpent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dragon#Comparative_mythology" title="Dragon">dragon</a> of some sort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995297&#8211;301_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995297&#8211;301-111">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference"
