id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006436&#8211;437_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006436&#8211;437-113">[110]</a></sup> Although the details of story often vary widely,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255-114">[111]</a></sup> in all iterations, several features remain remarkably the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255-114">[111]</a></sup> In iterations of the story, the serpent is usually associated with water in some way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;256_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;256-115">[112]</a></sup> The hero of the story is usually a <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">thunder-god</a> or a hero who is somehow associated with thunder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup> The serpent is usually multi-headed, or else "multiple" in some other way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006436&#8211;437_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006436&#8211;437-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_mythology" title="Hittite mythology">Hittite mythology</a>, the storm god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarhunt" title="Tarhunt">Tarhunt</a> slays the giant serpent <a href="/wiki/Illuyanka" title="Illuyanka">Illuyanka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, the god <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> slays the multi-headed serpent <a href="/wiki/Vritra" title="Vritra">Vritra</a>, which had been causing a drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;257_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;257-117">[114]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> slays the serpent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81liy%C4%81" title="K&#257;liy&#257;">K&#257;liy&#257;</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna,_South_Italy,_375-340_BC,_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3504" data-file-width="3824" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG/220px-Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG/330px-Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG/440px-Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna%2C_South_Italy%2C_375-340_BC%2C_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fragmentary_jar_with_scene_of_Herakles_slaying_the_Hydra_of_Lerna,_South_Italy,_375-340_BC,_ceramic_-_Fitchburg_Art_Museum_-_DSC08671.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek red-figure vase painting depicting <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> slaying the <a href="/wiki/Lernaean_Hydra" title="Lernaean Hydra">Lernaean Hydra</a>, c. 375&#8211;340 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Several variations of the story are also found in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007460_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007460-118">[115]</a></sup> The story is attested in the legend of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> slaying the hundred-headed <a href="/wiki/Typhon" title="Typhon">Typhon</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995448&#8211;460_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995448&#8211;460-119">[116]</a></sup> but it is also in the myths of the slaying of the nine-headed <a href="/wiki/Lernaean_Hydra" title="Lernaean Hydra">Lernaean Hydra</a> by <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> and the slaying of <a href="/wiki/Python_(mythology)" title="Python (mythology)">Python</a> by <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995460&#8211;464_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995460&#8211;464-120">[117]</a></sup> The story of <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>\'s theft of the cattle of <a href="/wiki/Geryon" title="Geryon">Geryon</a> is probably also related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup> Although Heracles is not usually thought of as a storm deity in the conventional sense, he bears many attributes held by other Indo-European storm deities, including physical strength and a knack for violence and gluttony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259_112-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007255&#8211;259-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995374&#8211;383_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995374&#8211;383-121">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original Proto-Indo-European myth is also reflected in <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995414&#8211;441_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995414&#8211;441-122">[119]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>, the god of thunder, slays the giant serpent <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6rmungandr" title="J&#246;rmungandr">J&#246;rmungandr</a>, which lived in the waters surrounding the realm of <a href="/wiki/Midgard" title="Midgard">Midgard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007259_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007259-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995429&#8211;441_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995429&#8211;441-124">[121]</a></sup> Other dragon-slaying myths are also found in the Germanic tradition. In the <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lsunga_saga" title="V&#246;lsunga saga">V&#246;lsunga saga</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Sigurd" title="Sigurd">Sigurd</a> slays the dragon <a href="/wiki/Fafnir" title="Fafnir">Fafnir</a> and, in <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>, the eponymous hero slays <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dragon_(Beowulf)" title="The Dragon (Beowulf)">a different dragon</a>.\n</p><p>Reflexes of the Proto-Indo-European dragon-slaying myth are found throughout other branches of the language family as well. In <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persian mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fereydun" title="Fereydun">Fereydun</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Garshasp" title="Garshasp">Garshasp</a>, slays <a href="/wiki/Zahhak" title="Zahhak">Zahhak</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perun" title="Perun">Perun</a>, the god of storms, slays <a href="/wiki/Veles_(god)" title="Veles (god)">Veles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dobrynya_Nikitich" title="Dobrynya Nikitich">Dobrynya Nikitich</a> slays the three-headed dragon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zmey" title="Zmey">Zmey</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Armenian_mythology" title="Armenian mythology">Armenian mythology</a>, the god <a href="/wiki/Vahagn" title="Vahagn">Vahagn</a> slays the dragon <a href="/wiki/Vishap" title="Vishap">Vishap</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurkjian1958_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurkjian1958-125">[122]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Folklore_of_Romania" title="Folklore of Romania">Romanian folklore</a>, <a href="/wiki/F%C4%83t-Frumos" title="F&#259;t-Frumos">F&#259;t-Frumos</a> slays the fire-spitting monster <a href="/wiki/Zmeu" title="Zmeu">Zmeu</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology" title="Celtic mythology">Celtic mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dian_Cecht" title="Dian Cecht">Dian Cecht</a> slays Meichi. The myth is believed to have symbolized a clash between forces of order and chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995299&#8211;300_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995299&#8211;300-126">[123]</a></sup> In every version of the story, the dragon or serpent always loses, although in some mythologies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse</a> <a href="/wiki/Ragnar%C3%B6k" title="Ragnar&#246;k">Ragnar&#246;k</a> myth, the hero or god dies as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995324&#8211;330_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkins1995324&#8211;330-127">[124]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twin_founders_2">Twin founders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Twin founders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The analysis of different Indo-European tales indicates that the Proto-Indo-Europeans believed there were two progenitors of mankind: *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Manu-</span></i> ("Man") and *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Yemo-</span></i> ("Twin"), his twin brother. A reconstructed creation myth involving the two is given by <a href="/wiki/David_W._Anthony" title="David W. Anthony">David W. Anthony</a>, attributed in part to <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lincoln" title="Bruce Lincoln">Bruce Lincoln</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135-70">[69]</a></sup> Manu and Yemo traverse the cosmos, accompanied by the primordial cow, and finally decide to create the world. To do so, Manu sacrifices either Yemo or the cow, and with help from the sky father, the storm god and the divine twins, forges the earth from the remains. Manu thus becomes the first priest and establishes the practice of sacrifice. The sky gods then present cattle to the third man, *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Trito</span></i>, who loses it to the three-headed <a href="#Dragon_or_Serpent">serpent</a> *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Ng<sup>w</sup>hi</span></i>, but eventually overcomes this monster either alone or aided by the sky father. Trito is now the first warrior and ensures that the cycle of mutual giving between gods and humans may continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135-70">[69]</a></sup> Reflexes of *Manu include Indic <a href="/wiki/Manu_(Hinduism)" title="Manu (Hinduism)">Manu</a>, Germanic <a href="/wiki/Mannus" title="Mannus">Mannus</a>; of Yemo, Indic <a href="/wiki/Yama" title="Yama">Yama</a>, Avestan <a href="/wiki/Jamshid" title="Jamshid">Yima</a>, Norse <a href="/wiki/Ymir" title="Ymir">Ymir</a>, possibly Roman <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Remus</a> (&lt; earlier <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> <i>*Yemos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007134&#8211;135-70">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg/330px-Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg/495px-Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg/660px-Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maria_Saal_Dom_Grabrelief_Romulus_und_Remus_27122013_774.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient Roman relief from the <a href="/wiki/Maria_Saal#Cathedral" title="Maria Saal">Cathedral of Maria Saal</a> showing the infant twins <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Romulus and Remus</a> being suckled by a she-wolf</div></div></div>\n<p>The early "history" of Rome is widely recognized as a historicized retelling of various old myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987144&#8211;165_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987144&#8211;165-128">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Romulus and Remus</a> are twin brothers from Roman mythology who both have stories in which they are killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;287_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;287-129">[126]</a></sup> The Roman writer Livy reports that Remus was believed to have been killed by his brother Romulus at the founding of Rome when they entered into a disagreement about which hill to build the city on. Later, Romulus himself is said to have been torn limb-from-limb by a group of senators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;290_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;290-130">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[d]</a></sup> Both of these myths are widely recognized as historicized remnants of the Proto-Indo-European creation story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;290_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987286&#8211;290-130">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Germanic languages have information about both Ymir and Mannus (reflexes of <i>*Yemo-</i> and <i>*Manu-</i> respectively),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285-132">[128]</a></sup> but they never appear together in the same myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285-132">[128]</a></sup> Instead, they only occur in myths widely separated by both time and circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285-132">[128]</a></sup> In chapter two of his book <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i>, which was written in Latin in around 98 A.D., the Roman writer <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> claims that Mannus, the son of Tuisto, was the ancestor of the Germanic peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987285-132">[128]</a></sup> This name never recurs anywhere in later Germanic literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200763&#8211;69_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200763&#8211;69-133">[129]</a></sup> but one proposed meaning of the continental Germanic tribal name <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alamanni" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a></i> is "<a href="/wiki/Mannus" title="Mannus">Mannus</a>\' own people" ("all-men" being another scholarly etymology).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200763&#8211;69_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200763&#8211;69-133">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_in_water">Fire in water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fire in water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another important possible myth is the myth of the fire in the waters, a myth which centers around the possible deity *<i>H<sub>2</sub>epom Nep&#333;ts</i>, a fiery deity who dwells in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006438_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006438-134">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987277&#8211;283_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987277&#8211;283-135">[131]</a></sup> In the Rigveda, the god <a href="/wiki/Apam_Napat" title="Apam Napat">Ap&#225;m N&#225;p&#225;t</a> is envisioned as a form of fire residing in the waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007270_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007270-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987277&#8211;279_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987277&#8211;279-137">[133]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology" title="Celtic mythology">Celtic mythology</a>, a well belonging to the god <a href="/wiki/Nechtan_(mythology)" title="Nechtan (mythology)">Nechtain</a> is said to blind all those who gaze into it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006438_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006438-134">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987279_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987279-138">[134]</a></sup> In an old Armenian poem, a small reed in the middle of the sea spontaneously catches fire and the hero <a href="/wiki/Vahagn" title="Vahagn">Vahagn</a> springs forth from it with fiery hair and a fiery beard and eyes that blaze as suns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007271_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007271-139">[135]</a></sup> In a ninth-century Norwegian poem by the poet Thiodolf, the name <i>s&#483;var ni&#254;r</i>, meaning "grandson of the sea," is used as a <a href="/wiki/Kenning" title="Kenning">kenning</a> for fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007272_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007272-140">[136]</a></sup> Even the Greek tradition contains possible allusions to the myth of a fire-god dwelling deep beneath the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007271_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007271-139">[135]</a></sup> The phrase "<i>&#957;&#941;&#960;&#959;&#948;&#949;&#962; &#954;&#945;&#955;&#8134;&#962; &#7945;&#955;&#959;&#963;&#973;&#948;&#957;&#951;&#962;</i>," meaning "descendants of the beautiful seas," is used in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey">The Odyssey</a></i> 4.404 as an epithet for the seals of <a href="/wiki/Proteus" title="Proteus">Proteus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007271_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007271-139">[135]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Binding_of_evil">Binding of evil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Binding of evil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Jaan Puhvel notes similarities between the Norse myth in which the god <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr" title="T&#253;r">T&#253;r</a> inserts his hand into the wolf <a href="/wiki/Fenrir" title="Fenrir">Fenrir</a>\'s mouth while the other gods bind him with <a href="/wiki/Gleipnir" title="Gleipnir">Gleipnir</a>, only for Fenrir to bite off T&#253;r\'s hand when he discovers he cannot break his bindings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987199_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987199-141">[137]</a></sup> and the Iranian myth in which <a href="/wiki/Jamshid" title="Jamshid">Jamshid</a> rescues his brother\'s corpse from <a href="/wiki/Ahriman" title="Ahriman">Ahriman</a>\'s bowels by reaching his hand up Ahriman\'s anus and pulling out his brother\'s corpse, only for his hand to become infected with <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119-142">[138]</a></sup> In both accounts, an authority figure forces the evil entity into submission by inserting his hand into the being\'s orifice (in Fenrir\'s case the mouth, in Ahriman\'s the anus) and losing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119-142">[138]</a></sup> Fenrir and Ahriman fulfill different roles in their own mythological traditions and are unlikely to be remnants of a Proto-Indo-European "evil god";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119&#8211;120_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987119&#8211;120-143">[139]</a></sup> nonetheless, it is clear that the "binding myth" is of Proto-Indo-European origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987120_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuhvel1987120-144">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmogony">Cosmogony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Cosmogony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">cosmogonic myths</a> of many Indo-European cultures a <a href="/wiki/World_egg" title="World egg">Cosmic Egg</a> symbolizes the primordial state from which the universe arises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underworld">Underworld</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Underworld">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attic_Red_Figure_(White_Ground)_Lekythos_with_Charon,_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter,_ca_500_-_450_BC,_Ashmolean_Museum,_Oxford,_UK_(22681344331).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4854" data-file-width="2808" decoding="async" height="380" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg/220px-Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg/330px-Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg/440px-Attic_Red_Figure_%28White_Ground%29_Lekythos_with_Charon%2C_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter%2C_ca_500_-_450_BC%2C_Ashmolean_Museum%2C_Oxford%2C_UK_%2822681344331%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attic_Red_Figure_(White_Ground)_Lekythos_with_Charon,_attributed_to_the_Tymbos_painter,_ca_500_-_450_BC,_Ashmolean_Museum,_Oxford,_UK_(22681344331).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Attic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-figure" title="Red-figure">red-figure</a> <a href="/wiki/Lekythos" title="Lekythos">lekythos</a> attributed to the Tymbos painter showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charon_(mythology)" title="Charon (mythology)">Charon</a> welcoming a soul into his boat, c. 500-450 BC</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Otherworld" title="Otherworld">Otherworld</a></div>\n<p>Most Indo-European traditions contain some kind of <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">Underworld</a> or <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">Afterlife</a>. It is possible that the Proto-Indo-Europeans may have believed that, in order to reach the Underworld, one needed to cross a river, guided by an old man (<i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/%C7%B5%C3%A9rh%E2%82%82onts" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Indo-European/&#501;&#233;rh&#8322;onts">*&#285;erh<sub>a</sub>ont-</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439-146">[142]</a></sup> The Greek tradition of the dead being ferried across the river <a href="/wiki/Styx" title="Styx">Styx</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charon_(mythology)" title="Charon (mythology)">Charon</a> is probably a reflex of this belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439-146">[142]</a></sup> The idea of crossing a river to reach the Underworld is also present throughout Celtic mythologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007390_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007390-147">[143]</a></sup> Several Vedic texts contain references to crossing a river in order to reach the land of the dead and the Latin word <i>tarentum</i> meaning "tomb" originally meant "crossing point."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007389_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007389-148">[144]</a></sup> In Norse mythology, Herm&#243;&#240;r must cross a bridge over the river Gi&#246;ll in order to reach <a href="/wiki/Hel_(being)" title="Hel (being)">Hel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007390&#8211;391_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007390&#8211;391-149">[145]</a></sup> In Latvian folk songs, the dead must cross a marsh rather than a river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007391_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007391-150">[146]</a></sup> Traditions of placing coins on the bodies of the deceased in order to pay the ferryman are attested in both ancient Greek and early modern Slavic funerary practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007390_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007390-147">[143]</a></sup> It is also possible that the Proto-Indo-Europeans may have believed that the Underworld was guarded by some kind of watchdog, similar to the Greek <a href="/wiki/Cerberus" title="Cerberus">Cerberus</a>, the Hindu &#346;&#225;rvara, or the Norse <a href="/wiki/Garmr" title="Garmr">Garmr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams2006439-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007392_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007392-151">[147]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_tree_and_serpent">World tree and serpent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: World tree and serpent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Proto-Indo-Europeans may have believed in some kind of world tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007346_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007346-152">[148]</a></sup> It is also possible that they may have believed that this tree was either guarded by or under constant attack from some kind of dragon or serpent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007346_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007346-152">[148]</a></sup> In Norse mythology, the cosmic tree <a href="/wiki/Yggdrasil" title="Yggdrasil">Yggdrasil</a> is tended by the three <a href="/wiki/Norns" title="Norns">Norns</a> while the dragon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nidhogg" title="Nidhogg">Nidhogg</a> gnaws at its roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007346_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007346-152">[148]</a></sup> In Greek mythology, the tree of the golden apples in the Garden of the Hesperides is tended by the three <a href="/wiki/Hesperides" title="Hesperides">Hesperides</a> and guarded by the hundred-headed dragon <a href="/wiki/Ladon_(mythology)" title="Ladon (mythology)">Ladon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007346&#8211;347_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007346&#8211;347-153">[149]</a></sup> In Indo-Iranian texts, there is a mythical tree dripping with <a href="/wiki/Soma_(drink)" title="Soma (drink)">Soma</a>, the immortal drink of the gods and, in later Pahlavi sources, a malicious lizard is said to lurk at the bottom of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007346_152-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007346-152">[148]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="713" decoding="async" height="21" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/32px-Draig.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/48px-Draig.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/64px-Draig.svg.png 2x" width="32"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Mythology" title="Portal:Mythology">Mythology portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">Interpretatio graeca</a></i>, the comparison of Greek deities to Germanic, Roman, and Celtic deities</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_religion" title="Neolithic religion">Neolithic religion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_society" title="Proto-Indo-European society">Proto-Indo-European society</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In order to present a consistent notation, the reconstructed forms used here are cited from <a href="#CITEREFMalloryAdams2006">Mallory &amp; Adams 2006</a>. For further explanation of the laryngeals &#8211; &lt;h<sub>1</sub>&gt;, &lt;h<sub>2</sub>&gt;, and &lt;h<sub>3</sub>&gt; &#8211; see the <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_theory" title="Laryngeal theory">Laryngeal theory</a> article.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The names of the individual Norns are given as <a href="/wiki/Ur%C3%B0r" title="Ur&#240;r">Ur&#240;r</a> ("Happened"), <a href="/wiki/Ver%C3%B0andi" title="Ver&#240;andi">Ver&#240;andi</a> ("Happening"), and <a href="/wiki/Skuld" title="Skuld">Skuld</a> ("Due"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup> but M. L. West notes that these names may be the result of classical influence from Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They also, most famously, appear as the <a href="/wiki/Three_Witches" title="Three Witches">Three Witches</a> in <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1606).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2007383_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2007383-94">[93]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One of the original sources for the stories of Romulus and Remus is Livy\'s <i>History of Rome</i>, vol. 1, parts iv&#8211;vii and xvi. This has been published in an Everyman edition, translated by W. M. Roberts, E. P. Dutton &amp; Co., New York 1912.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proto-Indo-European_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em;
